We are trying to find all N such that f(N)=N. So we need to consider the set of three largest proper divisors. The largest is N/p where p is the smallest prime factor. The second largest could be either N/q (where q is the second smallest prime factor) or N/p^2 if p^2|N and p^2 > p? Actually the second largest proper divisor is N divided by the smallest divisor >1 that is not p? Wait, the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor is N divided by the next smallest divisor of N after p. The divisors are all numbers of the form product of prime powers. The smallest divisor after 1 is p. The next smallest divisor is either p^2 (if p^2 divides N and p^2 is less than any other prime factor q) or q (if q is the next prime factor and q < p^2). So the second largest proper divisor is either N/p^2 or N/q. Then the third largest is N divided by the next smallest divisor after that, etc. So we can characterize f(N) in terms of the prime factorization.

Let N = ∏ p_i^{e_i} with p_1 < p_2 < ... < p_k. The divisors are numbers of the form ∏ p_i^{a_i} with 0 ≤ a_i ≤ e_i. The smallest divisors (excluding 1) are: p_1, then p_1^2 if e_1 ≥ 2, otherwise p_2 if k≥2, etc. So the sequence of the smallest divisors in increasing order: p_1, then if e_1 ≥ 2, p_1^2, else p_2; then if e_1 ≥ 3, p_1^3, else if e_2 ≥ 2, p_2^2, else p_3; etc. So the three largest proper divisors are N divided by the three smallest divisors (excluding 1). So if we let d_2, d_3, d_4 be the three smallest divisors greater than 1, then f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4. And we need f(N)=N.

Thus we need N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4 = N => 1/d_2 + 1/d_3 + 1/d_4 = 1.

So the condition f(N)=N is equivalent to: There exist three distinct divisors d_2, d_3, d_4 of N, each greater than 1, such that d_2 is the smallest divisor >1, d_3 the next smallest, d_4 the next smallest, and 1/d_2 + 1/d_3 + 1/d_4 = 1. And also N has at least these three proper divisors (i.e., N > d_4? Actually N itself is not among these, but N is larger than these). So N is a multiple of each d_i, but N itself is not necessarily among these d_i; they are proper divisors.

Thus the problem reduces to: Find all positive integers N with at least three proper divisors such that the three smallest divisors (excluding 1) satisfy that equation. And we need to consider the infinite orbit: Starting from a1, we apply f repeatedly. For the sequence to be infinite, we need that every term has at least three proper divisors, and also that f(N) never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. But note that if a term is a fixed point, it's fine. Also if a term leads to a cycle of fixed points? Actually if a term is not a fixed point, then a_{n+1} = f(N). For the sequence to be infinite, we need that all subsequent terms have at least three proper divisors. So we need to characterize all N such that the orbit under f avoids numbers with <3 proper divisors.

We can attempt to prove that the only numbers that have infinite orbits are those that are fixed points. But we have seen examples: a1=72 leads to 78 (fixed), so 72 works. So not only fixed points. So we need to find all a1 such that the sequence never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. Possibly the sequence eventually becomes constant (i.e., reaches a fixed point) or periodic with cycle consisting of numbers with at least three proper divisors. We need to find all a1 that avoid the "bad" numbers (numbers with less than 3 proper divisors). The set of numbers with less than 3 proper divisors: these are numbers that are either 1, prime, or product of two primes (including squares of primes? 4 has two proper divisors: 1,2 -> two, not allowed; 9 has 1,3 -> two; 25 has 1,5 -> two; 49 has 1,7 -> two; so squares of primes have exactly two proper divisors (1 and the prime). Also numbers like 6? 6 has three proper divisors, so allowed. So the "bad" numbers are: 1, primes, and numbers that are product of two primes (including squares of primes). But note that 1 has no proper divisors, so not allowed. Primes have exactly one proper divisor (1). Numbers with exactly two proper divisors are numbers that are either prime squares or product of two distinct primes. So the set B = {n: n is prime or n = p^2 or n = pq with p,q primes distinct}. Actually also n=1 is bad, but a1 must have at least three proper divisors, so a1 not in B. Also note that numbers like 12? 12 has 5 proper divisors, so allowed. So the condition for a term to be allowed is that it is not in B. So we need to find all a1 such that the orbit stays out of B.

We can try to analyze the function f on numbers not in B. Possibly f(N) is always in some set, and we can find all N that lead to a fixed point or cycle.

Alternatively, we can try to find all possible sequences. Now, the problem asks: Determine all possible values of a1 such that the infinite sequence exists. That means that starting from a1, the sequence defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n) is defined for all n. We need to find all a1 for which the orbit never hits B. Possibly the sequence always eventually becomes constant or periodic, but we need to check that it never hits B. We could try to prove that for any N not in B, f(N) is either in B or eventually reaches a fixed point? But we have seen that 72 leads to 78 (fixed), and 78 is fixed. Also 30 leads to 31 (prime). Since B is a set of "bad" numbers, we need to find all a1 that are not in B and whose iterates never fall into B. This is a kind of "preimage" analysis.

Let's denote the set of numbers that have infinite orbits (i.e., the sequence never hits a number with <3 proper divisors). Since the function f is deterministic, if an orbit ever hits a number that is in B, it terminates. So the orbit must avoid B forever. So the orbit must be contained in the complement of B. But note that the complement of B includes numbers that are either composite with at least three proper divisors, or maybe numbers that are not in B. Actually B is numbers with less than 3 proper divisors. So complement is numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. So the orbit must be a subset of that set.

Now, we have fixed points (f(N)=N) which are in the complement. Also, numbers that eventually map to a fixed point are in the complement if the entire forward orbit stays in the complement. For example, 72 maps to 78, which is fixed, so orbit: 72,78,78,... all in complement, so works. So we need to find all numbers N such that f^k(N) is never in B for any k≥0. Since B is "absorbing" in the sense that if we ever hit B, the sequence terminates; so we need that the forward orbit never enters B.

We can attempt to characterize the set S = {N: the orbit of N under f never hits B}. Since the problem asks for all possible a1, we need to find S ∩ {positive integers with at least three proper divisors}. But note that a1 must have at least three proper divisors, so a1 ∈ complement of B.

Observation: The function f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For N with at least three proper divisors, what can we say about f(N)? Possibly f(N) is always less than N? We need to find all a1 such that the forward orbit never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. This is reminiscent of a problem where the only numbers that avoid the "bad" set are those that are fixed points or eventually reach fixed points, and the initial values are exactly those numbers that are not in B and whose iterates never land in B. Let's analyze f(N) more structurally. For N with at least three proper divisors, let d2,d3,d4 be the three smallest divisors >1. Then f(N)=N/d2+N/d3+N/d4. We can also think of the largest proper divisors: the largest is N/d2, the second largest is N/d3, the third largest is N/d4. So a_{n+1} = N/d2+N/d3+N/d4.

Now, the condition for N to be in S (orbit avoids B) is that all iterates avoid B. Since B is defined by numbers with less than 3 proper divisors, we can characterize when f(N) is in B. That would be a condition on N.

Maybe we can find all N such that f(N) is prime or square of prime or product of two primes. That might be a finite set? We could try to characterize the "preimage" of B under f. That is, find all N such that f(N) ∈ B. Then if a1 is not in B and not in the preimage of B (i.e., f(a1) ∉ B), then we need to check further iterates. So we need to find all N such that f^k(N) ∉ B for all k. This is like the set of numbers that never hit B under iteration. Since B is "small" in some sense, maybe the only such numbers are those that are fixed points and numbers that map to fixed points in one step, and maybe also numbers that map to those in one step? But we can also have numbers that map to a fixed point after two steps? For instance, does any N map to 72? If 72 maps to 78, then we need to see if any N maps to 72. If N maps to 72, then the orbit would be N,72,78,78,... and if N has at least 3 proper divisors, then N works. Since fixed points are in that set, and numbers that eventually map to fixed points are also in that set if the entire forward orbit stays in complement. Let's try to find N systematically.

We can consider the possible values of s1 (smallest prime factor). Since f(N)=72, we have N/s1+N/s2+N/s3=72. Since s1 divides N, N is a multiple of s1. Also, N/s1 is an integer, and it's the largest proper divisor. So N/s1 ≤ 72 (since sum of three terms is 72, and all terms are positive). So N ≤ 72 * s1. For s1=2, N ≤ 144. For s1=3, N ≤ 216. For s1=5, N ≤ 360. etc. So N is bounded.

Now, we also need to consider the possibility of cycles that do not contain fixed points. Could there be a 2-cycle where f(N)=M and f(M)=N, with N and M both not fixed? Since f(N) is sum of three largest proper divisors, we can try to reason about the magnitude. For N not multiple of 6, f(N)<N. For N multiple of 6 but not fixed, f(N)>N. So if N is a fixed point, f(N)=N, so it's a 1-cycle. For a 2-cycle, we need that one of them is not multiple of 6, and the other is multiple of 6? Possibly N not multiple of 6, then f(N)<N. Because if f(N) is not multiple of 6, then f(f(N)) < f(N), so cannot equal N. So for a 2-cycle, one of them must be a multiple of 6 (the one that is the image of the other). So suppose N is not multiple of 6, then f(N) = M must be multiple of 6. And then M must satisfy f(M)=N. Since M is multiple of 6, if M is not fixed, then f(M)>M, so cannot equal N (which is less than M). So M must be a fixed point. So then N = f(M)=M, contradiction. So no 2-cycle. What about longer cycles? For a cycle of length >1, the function f must be such that the sequence alternates between going up and down.
